<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Nervous system</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Part of an animal that coordinates actions and senses</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Nervous_system_(disambiguation)" title="Nervous system (disambiguation)">Nervous system (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1066479718">.mw-parser-output class="infobox-data"><a class="external text" href="https://meshb.nlm.nih.gov/record/ui?ui=D009420" rel="nofollow">D009420</a></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row" <p>In <a href="/wiki/Biology" title="Biology">biology</a>, the classical doctrine of the <b>nervous system</b> determines that  it is a <a href="/wiki/Complex_system" title="Complex system">highly complex</a> part of an <a href="/wiki/Animal" title="Animal">animal</a> that coordinates its <a href="/wiki/Behavior" title="Behavior">actions</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sense" title="Sense">sensory</a> information by transmitting <a href="/wiki/Action_potential" title="Action potential">signals</a> to and from different parts of its body. The nervous system detects environmental changes that impact the body, then works in tandem with the <a href="/wiki/Endocrine_system" title="Endocrine system">endocrine system</a> to respond to such events.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Nervous_tissue" title="Nervous tissue">Nervous tissue</a> first arose in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ediacara_biota" title="Ediacara biota">wormlike organisms</a> about 550 to 600 million years ago. However, this classical doctrine has been challenged in recent decades by discoveries about the existence and use of electrical signals in plants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> On the basis of these findings, some scientists have  proposed that a plant nervous system exists and that a scientific field called plant neurobiology should be created.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> This proposal has led to a dispute in the scientific community between those who think we should talk about the nervous system of plants and those who are against it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> The inflexibility of the positions in the scientific debate on both sides has led to the proposal of a solution to the debate, consisting of redefining the concept of the nervous system by using only physiological criteria and avoiding phylogenetic criteria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>In vertebrates it consists of two main parts, the <a href="/wiki/Central_nervous_system" title="Central nervous system">central nervous system</a> (CNS) and the <a href="/wiki/Peripheral_nervous_system" title="Peripheral nervous system">peripheral nervous system</a> (PNS). The CNS consists of the <a href="/wiki/Brain" title="Brain">brain</a> and <a href="/wiki/Spinal_cord" title="Spinal cord">spinal cord</a>. The PNS consists mainly of <a href="/wiki/Nerve" title="Nerve">nerves</a>, which are enclosed bundles of the long fibers or <a href="/wiki/Axon" title="Axon">axons</a>, that connect the CNS to every other part of the body. Nerves that transmit signals from the brain are called <a href="/wiki/Motor_nerve" title="Motor nerve">motor nerves</a> or <i><a href="/wiki/Efferent_nerve_fiber" title="Efferent nerve fiber">efferent</a></i> nerves, while those nerves that transmit information from the body to the CNS are called <a href="/wiki/Sensory_nerve" title="Sensory nerve">sensory nerves</a> or <i><a href="/wiki/Afferent_nerve_fiber" title="Afferent nerve fiber">afferent</a></i>. <a href="/wiki/Spinal_nerve" title="Spinal nerve">Spinal nerves</a> are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mixed_nerve" title="Mixed nerve">mixed nerves</a> that serve both functions. The PNS is divided into three separate subsystems, the <a href="/wiki/Somatic_nervous_system" title="Somatic nervous system">somatic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Autonomic_nervous_system" title="Autonomic nervous system">autonomic</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Enteric_nervous_system" title="Enteric nervous system">enteric</a> nervous systems. Somatic nerves mediate voluntary movement. The autonomic nervous system is further subdivided into the <a href="/wiki/Sympathetic_nervous_system" title="Sympathetic nervous system">sympathetic</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Parasympathetic_nervous_system" title="Parasympathetic nervous system">parasympathetic</a> nervous systems. The sympathetic nervous system is activated in cases of emergencies to mobilize energy, while the parasympathetic nervous system is activated when organisms are in a relaxed state. The enteric nervous system functions to control the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gastrointestinal" title="Gastrointestinal">gastrointestinal</a> system. Both autonomic and enteric nervous systems function involuntarily. Nerves that exit from the cranium are called <a href="/wiki/Cranial_nerves" title="Cranial nerves">cranial nerves</a> while those exiting from the spinal cord are called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spinal_nerves" title="Spinal nerves">spinal nerves</a>.
</p><p>At the cellular level, the nervous system is defined by the presence of a special type of cell, called the <a href="/wiki/Neuron" title="Neuron">neuron</a>, also known as a "nerve cell." Neurons have special structures that allow them to send signals rapidly and precisely to other cells. They send these signals in the form of electrochemical impulses traveling along thin fibers called <a href="/wiki/Axon" title="Axon">axons</a>, which can be directly transmitted to neighboring cells through <a href="/wiki/Electrical_synapse" title="Electrical synapse">electrical synapses</a> or cause chemicals called <a href="/wiki/Neurotransmitter" title="Neurotransmitter">neurotransmitters</a> to be released at <a href="/wiki/Chemical_synapse" title="Chemical synapse">chemical synapses</a>. A cell that receives a synaptic signal from a neuron may be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cell_excitability" title="Cell excitability">excited</a>, inhibited, or otherwise <a href="/wiki/Neuromodulation" title="Neuromodulation">modulated</a>. The connections between neurons can form <a href="/wiki/Neural_pathway" title="Neural pathway">neural pathways</a>, <a href="/wiki/Neural_circuit" title="Neural circuit">neural circuits</a>, and larger <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Large_scale_brain_networks" title="Large scale brain networks">networks</a> that generate an organism's perception of the world and determine its behavior. Along with neurons, the nervous system contains other specialized cells called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neuroglia" title="Neuroglia">glial cells</a> (or simply glia), which provide structural and metabolic support.
</p><p>Nervous systems are found in most multicellular animals, but vary greatly in complexity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Columbia_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Columbia-8">[8]</a></sup> The only multicellular animals that have no nervous system at all are <a href="/wiki/Sponge" title="Sponge">sponges</a>, <a href="/wiki/Placozoa" title="Placozoa">placozoans</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mesozoa" title="Mesozoa">mesozoans</a>, which have very simple body plans. The nervous systems of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radial_symmetry" title="Radial symmetry">radially symmetric</a> organisms <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ctenophores" title="Ctenophores">ctenophores</a> (comb jellies) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cnidarians" title="Cnidarians">cnidarians</a> (which include <a href="/wiki/Sea_anemone" title="Sea anemone">anemones</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydras" title="Hydras">hydras</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Corals" title="Corals">corals</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jellyfish" title="Jellyfish">jellyfish</a>) consist of a diffuse <a href="/wiki/Nerve_net" title="Nerve net">nerve net</a>. All other animal species, with the exception of a few types of worms, have a nervous system containing a brain, a central cord (or two cords running in <a href="/wiki/Parallel_(geometry)" title="Parallel (geometry)">parallel</a>), and nerves radiating from the brain and central cord. The size of the nervous system ranges from a few hundred cells in the simplest worms, to around 300 billion cells in African elephants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p><p>The central nervous system functions to send signals from one cell to others, or from one part of the body to others and to receive feedback. Malfunction of the nervous system can occur as a result of genetic defects, physical damage due to trauma or toxicity, infection, or simply <a href="/wiki/Senescence" title="Senescence">senesence</a>. The medical specialty of <a href="/wiki/Neurology" title="Neurology">neurology</a> studies disorders of the nervous system and looks for interventions that can prevent or treat them.  In the peripheral nervous system, the most common problem is the failure of nerve conduction, which can be due to different causes including <a href="/wiki/Diabetic_neuropathy" title="Diabetic neuropathy">diabetic neuropathy</a> and demyelinating disorders such as <a href="/wiki/Multiple_sclerosis" title="Multiple sclerosis">multiple sclerosis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Amyotrophic_lateral_sclerosis" title="Amyotrophic lateral sclerosis">amyotrophic lateral sclerosis</a>. <a href="/wiki/Neuroscience" title="Neuroscience">Neuroscience</a> is the field of science that focuses on the study of the nervous system.
</p>

</ul>
</div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Structure">Structure</span></h2>
<p>The nervous system derives its name from nerves, which are cylindrical bundles of fibers (the <a href="/wiki/Axon" title="Axon">axons</a> of <a href="/wiki/Neuron" title="Neuron">neurons</a>), that emanate from the brain and <a href="/wiki/Spinal_cord" title="Spinal cord">spinal cord</a>, and branch repeatedly to innervate every part of the body.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KandelCh2_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-KandelCh2-10">[10]</a></sup> Nerves are large enough to have been recognized by the ancient Egyptians, Greeks, and Romans,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FingerCh1_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-FingerCh1-11">[11]</a></sup> but their internal structure was not understood until it became possible to examine them using a microscope.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> The author Michael Nikoletseas wrote:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p>
<blockquote><p>It is difficult to believe that until approximately year 1900 it was not known that neurons are the basic units of the brain (<a href="/wiki/Santiago_Ram%C3%B3n_y_Cajal" title="Santiago Ramón y Cajal">Santiago Ramón y Cajal</a>). Equally surprising is the fact that the concept of chemical transmission in the brain was not known until around 1930 (<a href="/wiki/Henry_Hallett_Dale" title="Henry Hallett Dale">Henry Hallett Dale</a> and <a href="/wiki/Otto_Loewi" title="Otto Loewi">Otto Loewi</a>). We began to understand the basic electrical phenomenon that neurons use in order to communicate among themselves, the action potential, in the 1950s (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alan_Lloyd_Hodgkin" title="Alan Lloyd Hodgkin">Alan Lloyd Hodgkin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Huxley" title="Andrew Huxley">Andrew Huxley</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_Eccles_(neurophysiologist)" title="John Eccles (neurophysiologist)">John Eccles</a>). It was in the 1960s that we became aware of how basic neuronal networks code stimuli and thus basic concepts are possible (<a href="/wiki/David_H._Hubel" title="David H. Hubel">David H. Hubel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Torsten_Wiesel" title="Torsten Wiesel">Torsten Wiesel</a>). The molecular revolution swept across US universities in the 1980s. It was in the 1990s that molecular mechanisms of behavioral phenomena became widely known (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eric_Richard_Kandel" title="Eric Richard Kandel">Eric Richard Kandel</a>).</p></blockquote>
<p>A microscopic examination shows that nerves consist primarily of axons, along with different membranes that wrap around them and segregate them into <a href="/wiki/Nerve_fascicle" title="Nerve fascicle">fascicles</a>. The neurons that give rise to nerves do not lie entirely within the nerves themselves—their cell bodies reside within the brain, <a href="/wiki/Spinal_cord" title="Spinal cord">spinal cord</a>, or peripheral <a href="/wiki/Ganglion" title="Ganglion">ganglia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KandelCh2_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-KandelCh2-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p><p>All animals more advanced than sponges have nervous systems. However, even <a href="/wiki/Sponge" title="Sponge">sponges</a>, unicellular animals, and non-animals such as slime molds have cell-to-cell signalling mechanisms that are precursors to those of neurons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sakarya_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sakarya-14">[14]</a></sup> In radially symmetric animals such as the jellyfish and hydra, the nervous system consists of a <a href="/wiki/Nerve_net" title="Nerve net">nerve net</a>, a diffuse network of isolated cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ruppert_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ruppert-15">[15]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Bilateria" title="Bilateria">bilaterian</a> animals, which make up the great majority of existing species, the nervous system has a common structure that originated early in the <a href="/wiki/Ediacaran" title="Ediacaran">Ediacaran</a> period, over 550 million years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Balavoine_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Balavoine-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cells">Cells</span></h3>
<p>The nervous system contains two main categories or types of cells: <a href="/wiki/Neuron" title="Neuron">neurons</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glial_cell" title="Glial cell">glial cells</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Neurons">Neurons</span></h4>
<table class="infobox">
<caption>Structure of a typical neuron
</caption>
<tbody><tr>
<th style="background-color: #d0d0d0;">Neuron
</th></tr>
<tr style="text-align: center">
<td><div style="position: relative;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Neuron_Hand-tuned.svg"><img alt="At one end of an elongated structure is a branching mass. At the centre of this mass is the nucleus and the branches are dendrites. A thick axon trails away from the mass, ending with further branching which are labeled as axon terminals. Along the axon are a number of protuberances labeled as myelin sheaths." data-file-height="634" data-file-width="1179" decoding="async" height="215" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Neuron_Hand-tuned.svg/400px-Neuron_Hand-tuned.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Neuron_Hand-tuned.svg/600px-Neuron_Hand-tuned.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Neuron_Hand-tuned.svg/800px-Neuron_Hand-tuned.svg.png 2x" width="400"/></a>
<div style="position:absolute; left:50px; top:2px"><a href="/wiki/Dendrite" title="Dendrite">Dendrite</a></div>
<div style="position:absolute; left:135px; top:60px"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perikaryon" title="Perikaryon">Soma</a></div>
<div style="position:absolute; left:189px; top:116px"><a href="/wiki/Axon" title="Axon">Axon</a></div>
<div style="position:absolute; left:13px; top:197px"><a href="/wiki/Cell_nucleus" title="Cell nucleus">Nucleus</a></div>
<div style="position:absolute; left:226px; top:38px"><a href="/wiki/Node_of_Ranvier" title="Node of Ranvier">Node of<br/>Ranvier</a></div>
<div style="position:absolute; left:319px; top:0px"><a href="/wiki/Axon_terminal" title="Axon terminal">Axon terminal</a></div>
<div style="position:absolute; left:306px; top:161px"><a href="/wiki/Schwann_cell" title="Schwann cell">Schwann cell</a></div>
<div style="position:absolute; left:220px; top:187px"><a href="/wiki/Myelin" title="Myelin">Myelin sheath</a></div></div>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The nervous system is defined by the presence of a special type of cell—the <a href="/wiki/Neuron" title="Neuron">neuron</a> (sometimes called "neurone" or "nerve cell").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KandelCh2_10-2"><a href="#cite_note-KandelCh2-10">[10]</a></sup> Neurons can be distinguished from other cells in a number of ways, but their most fundamental property is that they communicate with other cells via <a href="/wiki/Synapse" title="Synapse">synapses</a>, which are membrane-to-membrane junctions containing molecular machinery that allows rapid transmission of signals, either electrical or chemical.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KandelCh2_10-3"><a href="#cite_note-KandelCh2-10">[10]</a></sup> Many types of neuron possess an <a href="/wiki/Axon" title="Axon">axon</a>, a protoplasmic protrusion that can extend to distant parts of the body and make thousands of synaptic contacts;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KandelCh4_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-KandelCh4-18">[18]</a></sup> axons typically extend throughout the body in bundles called nerves.
</p><p>Even in the nervous system of a single species such as humans, hundreds of different types of neurons exist, with a wide variety of morphologies and functions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KandelCh4_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-KandelCh4-18">[18]</a></sup> These include <a href="/wiki/Sensory_neuron" title="Sensory neuron">sensory neurons</a> that transmute physical stimuli such as light and sound into neural signals, and <a href="/wiki/Motor_neuron" title="Motor neuron">motor neurons</a> that transmute neural signals into activation of muscles or glands; however in many species the great majority of neurons participate in the formation of centralized structures (the brain and ganglia) and they receive all of their input from other neurons and send their output to other neurons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KandelCh2_10-4"><a href="#cite_note-KandelCh2-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Glial_cells">Glial cells</span></h4>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glial_cell" title="Glial cell">Glial cells</a> (named from the Greek for "glue") are non-neuronal cells that provide support and <a href="/wiki/Nutrition" title="Nutrition">nutrition</a>, maintain <a href="/wiki/Homeostasis" title="Homeostasis">homeostasis</a>, form <a href="/wiki/Myelin" title="Myelin">myelin</a>, and participate in signal transmission in the nervous system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Allen2009_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Allen2009-19">[19]</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Human_brain" title="Human brain">human brain</a>, it is estimated that the total number of glia roughly equals the number of neurons, although the proportions vary in different brain areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Azevedo_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Azevedo-20">[20]</a></sup> Among the most important functions of glial cells are to support neurons and hold them in place; to supply nutrients to neurons; to insulate neurons electrically; to destroy <a href="/wiki/Pathogen" title="Pathogen">pathogens</a> and remove dead neurons; and to provide guidance cues directing the axons of neurons to their targets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Allen2009_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-Allen2009-19">[19]</a></sup> A very important type of glial cell (<a href="/wiki/Oligodendrocyte" title="Oligodendrocyte">oligodendrocytes</a> in the central nervous system, and <a href="/wiki/Schwann_cell" title="Schwann cell">Schwann cells</a> in the peripheral nervous system) generates layers of a fatty substance called <a href="/wiki/Myelin" title="Myelin">myelin</a> that wraps around axons and provides electrical insulation which allows them to transmit action potentials much more rapidly and efficiently. Recent findings indicate that glial cells, such as microglia and astrocytes, serve as important resident immune cells within the central nervous system.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Anatomy_in_vertebrates">Anatomy in vertebrates</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_nerves_of_the_human_body" title="List of nerves of the human body">List of nerves of the human body</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_regions_in_the_human_brain" title="List of regions in the human brain">List of regions in the human brain</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:382px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NSdiagram.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="408" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="303" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/NSdiagram.svg/380px-NSdiagram.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/NSdiagram.svg/570px-NSdiagram.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/NSdiagram.svg/760px-NSdiagram.svg.png 2x" width="380"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:NSdiagram.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Diagram showing the major divisions of the vertebrate nervous system.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Visible_Human_head_slice.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="590" data-file-width="468" decoding="async" height="277" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Visible_Human_head_slice.jpg/220px-Visible_Human_head_slice.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Visible_Human_head_slice.jpg/330px-Visible_Human_head_slice.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Visible_Human_head_slice.jpg/440px-Visible_Human_head_slice.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Visible_Human_head_slice.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Horizontal section of the head of an adult female human, showing skin, skull, and brain with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gray_matter" title="Gray matter">gray matter</a> (brown in this image) and underlying <a href="/wiki/White_matter" title="White matter">white matter</a></div></div></div>
<p>The nervous system of <a href="/wiki/Vertebrate" title="Vertebrate">vertebrates</a> (including humans) is divided into the <a href="/wiki/Central_nervous_system" title="Central nervous system">central nervous system</a> (CNS) and the <a href="/wiki/Peripheral_nervous_system" title="Peripheral nervous system">peripheral nervous system</a> (PNS).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KandelCh17_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-KandelCh17-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>The (CNS) is the major division, and consists of the <a href="/wiki/Brain" title="Brain">brain</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Spinal_cord" title="Spinal cord">spinal cord</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KandelCh17_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-KandelCh17-21">[21]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Spinal_canal" title="Spinal canal">spinal canal</a> contains the spinal cord, while the <a href="/wiki/Cranial_cavity" title="Cranial cavity">cranial cavity</a> contains the brain.  The CNS is enclosed and protected by the <a href="/wiki/Meninges" title="Meninges">meninges</a>, a three-layered system of membranes, including a tough, leathery outer layer called the <a href="/wiki/Dura_mater" title="Dura mater">dura mater</a>. The brain is also protected by the skull, and the spinal cord by the <a href="/wiki/Vertebra" title="Vertebra">vertebrae</a>.
</p><p>The peripheral nervous system (PNS) is a collective term for the nervous system structures that do not lie within the CNS.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gray233_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gray233-22">[22]</a></sup> The large majority of the axon bundles called nerves are considered to belong to the PNS, even when the cell bodies of the neurons to which they belong reside within the brain or spinal cord.  The PNS is divided into <a href="/wiki/Somatic_nervous_system" title="Somatic nervous system">somatic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Autonomic_nervous_system" title="Autonomic nervous system">visceral</a> parts.  The somatic part consists of the nerves that innervate the skin, joints, and muscles.  The cell bodies of somatic sensory neurons lie in <a href="/wiki/Dorsal_root_ganglion" title="Dorsal root ganglion">dorsal root ganglia</a> of the spinal cord.  The visceral part, also known as the autonomic nervous system, contains neurons that innervate the internal organs, blood vessels, and glands.  The autonomic nervous system itself consists of two parts: the <a href="/wiki/Sympathetic_nervous_system" title="Sympathetic nervous system">sympathetic nervous system</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Parasympathetic_nervous_system" title="Parasympathetic nervous system">parasympathetic nervous system</a>.  Some authors also include sensory neurons whose cell bodies lie in the periphery (for senses such as hearing) as part of the PNS; others, however, omit them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hubbardvii_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hubbardvii-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p><p>The vertebrate nervous system can also be divided into areas called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gray_matter" title="Gray matter">gray matter</a> and <a href="/wiki/White_matter" title="White matter">white matter</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Purves15_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Purves15-24">[24]</a></sup> Gray matter (which is only gray in preserved tissue, and is better described as pink or light brown in living tissue) contains a high proportion of cell bodies of neurons.  White matter is composed mainly of <a href="/wiki/Myelin" title="Myelin">myelinated</a> axons, and takes its color from the myelin.  White matter includes all of the nerves, and much of the interior of the brain and spinal cord.  Gray matter is found in clusters of neurons in the brain and spinal cord, and in cortical layers that line their surfaces.  There is an anatomical convention that a cluster of neurons in the brain or spinal cord is called a <a href="/wiki/Nucleus_(neuroanatomy)" title="Nucleus (neuroanatomy)">nucleus</a>, whereas a cluster of neurons in the periphery is called a <a href="/wiki/Ganglion" title="Ganglion">ganglion</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DorlandsGanglion_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-DorlandsGanglion-25">[25]</a></sup> There are, however, a few exceptions to this rule, notably including the part of the forebrain called the <a href="/wiki/Basal_ganglia" title="Basal ganglia">basal ganglia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Afifi_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Afifi-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Comparative_anatomy_and_evolution">Comparative anatomy and evolution</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Evolution_of_nervous_systems" title="Evolution of nervous systems">Evolution of nervous systems</a></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Neural_precursors_in_sponges">Neural precursors in sponges</span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Sponge" title="Sponge">Sponges</a> have no cells connected to each other by <a href="/wiki/Synapse" title="Synapse">synaptic junctions</a>, that is, no neurons, and therefore no nervous system. They do, however, have <a href="/wiki/Homology_(biology)" title="Homology (biology)">homologs</a> of many genes that play key roles in synaptic function. Recent studies have shown that sponge cells express a group of proteins that cluster together to form a structure resembling a <a href="/wiki/Postsynaptic_density" title="Postsynaptic density">postsynaptic density</a> (the signal-receiving part of a synapse).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sakarya_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sakarya-14">[14]</a></sup> However, the function of this structure is currently unclear. Although sponge cells do not show synaptic transmission, they do communicate with each other via calcium waves and other impulses, which mediate some simple actions such as whole-body contraction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jacobs_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jacobs-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Radiata">Radiata</span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Cnidaria" title="Cnidaria">Jellyfish</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ctenophore" title="Ctenophore">comb jellies</a>, and related animals have diffuse nerve nets rather than a central nervous system.  In most jellyfish the nerve net is spread more or less evenly across the body; in comb jellies it is concentrated near the mouth.  The nerve nets consist of sensory neurons, which pick up chemical, tactile, and visual signals; motor neurons, which can activate contractions of the body wall; and intermediate neurons, which detect patterns of activity in the sensory neurons and, in response, send signals to groups of motor neurons.  In some cases groups of intermediate neurons are clustered into discrete <a href="/wiki/Ganglion" title="Ganglion">ganglia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ruppert_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ruppert-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p>The development of the nervous system in <a href="/wiki/Radiata" title="Radiata">radiata</a> is relatively unstructured.  Unlike <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bilaterians" title="Bilaterians">bilaterians</a>, radiata only have two primordial cell layers, <a href="/wiki/Endoderm" title="Endoderm">endoderm</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ectoderm" title="Ectoderm">ectoderm</a>.  Neurons are generated from a special set of ectodermal precursor cells, which also serve as precursors for every other ectodermal cell type.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sanes3_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sanes3-28">[28]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Bilateria">Bilateria</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bilaterian-plan.svg"><img alt="A rod-shaped body contains a digestive system running from the mouth at one end to the anus at the other. Alongside the digestive system is a nerve cord with a brain at the end, near to the mouth." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="218" data-file-width="744" decoding="async" height="64" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Bilaterian-plan.svg/220px-Bilaterian-plan.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Bilaterian-plan.svg/330px-Bilaterian-plan.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Bilaterian-plan.svg/440px-Bilaterian-plan.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bilaterian-plan.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Nervous system of a bilaterian animal, in the form of a nerve cord with segmental enlargements, and a "brain" at the front</div></div></div>
<p>The vast majority of existing animals are <a href="/wiki/Bilateria" title="Bilateria">bilaterians</a>, meaning animals with left and right sides that are approximate mirror images of each other.  All bilateria are thought to have descended from a common wormlike ancestor that appeared in the Ediacaran period, 550–600 million years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Balavoine_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-Balavoine-16">[16]</a></sup> The fundamental bilaterian body form is a tube with a hollow gut cavity running from mouth to anus, and a nerve cord with an enlargement (a "ganglion") for each body segment, with an especially large ganglion at the front, called the "brain".
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:127px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gray797.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="850" data-file-width="418" decoding="async" height="254" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Gray797.png/125px-Gray797.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Gray797.png/188px-Gray797.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Gray797.png/250px-Gray797.png 2x" width="125"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gray797.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Area of the human body surface innervated by each spinal nerve</div></div></div>
<p>Even mammals, including humans, show the segmented bilaterian body plan at the level of the nervous system.  The spinal cord contains a series of segmental ganglia, each giving rise to motor and sensory nerves that innervate a portion of the body surface and underlying musculature.  On the limbs, the layout of the innervation pattern is complex, but on the trunk it gives rise to a series of narrow bands.  The top three segments belong to the brain, giving rise to the forebrain, midbrain, and hindbrain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ghysen_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ghysen-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p><p>Bilaterians can be divided, based on events that occur very early in embryonic development, into two groups (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Superphylum" title="Superphylum">superphyla</a>) called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protostomia" title="Protostomia">protostomes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Deuterostome" title="Deuterostome">deuterostomes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Erwin_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Erwin-30">[30]</a></sup> Deuterostomes include vertebrates as well as <a href="/wiki/Echinoderm" title="Echinoderm">echinoderms</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hemichordata" title="Hemichordata">hemichordates</a> (mainly acorn worms), and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Xenoturbellida" title="Xenoturbellida">Xenoturbellidans</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bourlat_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bourlat-31">[31]</a></sup> Protostomes, the more diverse group, include <a href="/wiki/Arthropod" title="Arthropod">arthropods</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mollusc" title="Mollusc">molluscs</a>, and numerous types of worms.  There is a basic difference between the two groups in the placement of the nervous system within the body:  protostomes possess a nerve cord on the ventral (usually bottom) side of the body, whereas in deuterostomes the nerve cord is on the dorsal (usually top) side.  In fact, numerous aspects of the body are inverted between the two groups, including the expression patterns of several genes that show dorsal-to-ventral gradients.  Most anatomists now consider that the bodies of protostomes and deuterostomes are "flipped over" with respect to each other, a hypothesis that was first proposed by <a href="/wiki/%C3%89tienne_Geoffroy_Saint-Hilaire" title="Étienne Geoffroy Saint-Hilaire">Geoffroy Saint-Hilaire</a> for insects in comparison to vertebrates.  Thus insects, for example, have nerve cords that run along the ventral midline of the body, while all vertebrates have spinal cords that run along the dorsal midline.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lichtneckert_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lichtneckert-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Worms">Worms</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Earthworm_nervous_system.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="442" data-file-width="674" decoding="async" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Earthworm_nervous_system.png/250px-Earthworm_nervous_system.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Earthworm_nervous_system.png/375px-Earthworm_nervous_system.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Earthworm_nervous_system.png/500px-Earthworm_nervous_system.png 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Earthworm_nervous_system.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Earthworm nervous system. <i>Top:</i> side view of the front of the worm. <i>Bottom:</i> nervous system in isolation, viewed from above</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Worm" title="Worm">Worms</a> are the simplest bilaterian animals, and reveal the basic structure of the bilaterian nervous system in the most straightforward way.  As an example, <a href="/wiki/Earthworm" title="Earthworm">earthworms</a> have dual <a href="/wiki/Ventral_nerve_cord" title="Ventral nerve cord">nerve cords</a> running along the length of the body and merging at the tail and the mouth. These nerve cords are connected by <a href="/wiki/Transverse_plane" title="Transverse plane">transverse</a> nerves like the rungs of a ladder. These transverse nerves help <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coordinate" title="Coordinate">coordinate</a> the two sides of the animal. Two <a href="/wiki/Ganglion" title="Ganglion">ganglia</a> at the head (the "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nerve_ring" title="Nerve ring">nerve ring</a>") end function similar to a simple <a href="/wiki/Brain" title="Brain">brain</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Simple_eyes_in_arthropods" title="Simple eyes in arthropods">Photoreceptors</a> on the animal's eyespots provide sensory information on light and dark.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Adey_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Adey-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p><p>The nervous system of one very small roundworm, the <a href="/wiki/Nematode" title="Nematode">nematode</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Caenorhabditis_elegans" title="Caenorhabditis elegans">Caenorhabditis elegans</a></i>, has been completely mapped out in a <a href="/wiki/Connectome" title="Connectome">connectome</a> including its synapses. Every neuron and its <a href="/wiki/Fate_mapping" title="Fate mapping">cellular lineage</a> has been recorded and most, if not all, of the neural connections are known. In this species, the nervous system is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sexually_dimorphic" title="Sexually dimorphic">sexually dimorphic</a>; the nervous systems of the two sexes, males and female <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hermaphrodites" title="Hermaphrodites">hermaphrodites</a>, have different numbers of neurons and groups of neurons that perform sex-specific functions. In <i>C. elegans</i>, males have exactly 383 neurons, while hermaphrodites have exactly 302 neurons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wormbook_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wormbook-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Arthropods">Arthropods</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Spider_internal_anatomy-en.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="503" data-file-width="1148" decoding="async" height="110" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Spider_internal_anatomy-en.svg/250px-Spider_internal_anatomy-en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Spider_internal_anatomy-en.svg/375px-Spider_internal_anatomy-en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Spider_internal_anatomy-en.svg/500px-Spider_internal_anatomy-en.svg.png 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Spider_internal_anatomy-en.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Internal anatomy of a spider, showing the nervous system in blue</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Arthropod" title="Arthropod">Arthropods</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Insect" title="Insect">insects</a> and <a href="/wiki/Crustacean" title="Crustacean">crustaceans</a>, have a nervous system made up of a series of <a href="/wiki/Ganglion" title="Ganglion">ganglia</a>, connected by a <a href="/wiki/Ventral_nerve_cord" title="Ventral nerve cord">ventral nerve cord</a> made up of two parallel connectives running along the length of the <a href="/wiki/Abdomen" title="Abdomen">belly</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapman_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chapman-35">[35]</a></sup> Typically, each body segment has one <a href="/wiki/Ganglion" title="Ganglion">ganglion</a> on each side, though some ganglia are fused to form the brain and other large ganglia.  The head segment contains the brain, also known as the <a href="/wiki/Supraesophageal_ganglion" title="Supraesophageal ganglion">supraesophageal ganglion</a>. In the <a href="/wiki/Insect#Nervous_system" title="Insect">insect nervous system</a>, the brain is anatomically divided into the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protocerebrum" title="Protocerebrum">protocerebrum</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deutocerebrum" title="Deutocerebrum">deutocerebrum</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tritocerebrum" title="Tritocerebrum">tritocerebrum</a>. Immediately behind the brain is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Subesophageal_ganglion" title="Subesophageal ganglion">subesophageal ganglion</a>, which is composed of three pairs of fused ganglia.  It controls the <a href="/wiki/Arthropod_mouthparts" title="Arthropod mouthparts">mouthparts</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Salivary_glands" title="Salivary glands">salivary glands</a> and certain <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muscle" title="Muscle">muscles</a>.  Many arthropods have well-developed <a href="/wiki/Sense" title="Sense">sensory</a> organs, including <a href="/wiki/Compound_eye" title="Compound eye">compound eyes</a> for vision and <a href="/wiki/Antenna_(biology)" title="Antenna (biology)">antennae</a> for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Olfaction" title="Olfaction">olfaction</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pheromone" title="Pheromone">pheromone</a> sensation. The sensory information from these organs is processed by the brain.
</p><p>In insects, many neurons have cell bodies that are positioned at the edge of the brain and are electrically passive—the cell bodies serve only to provide metabolic support and do not participate in signalling.  A protoplasmic fiber runs from the cell body and branches profusely, with some parts transmitting signals and other parts receiving signals.  Thus, most parts of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Insect_brain" title="Insect brain">insect brain</a> have passive cell bodies arranged around the periphery, while the neural signal processing takes place in a tangle of protoplasmic fibers called <a href="/wiki/Neuropil" title="Neuropil">neuropil</a>, in the interior.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id=".22Identified.22_neurons"></span><span class="mw-headline" id='"Identified"_neurons'>"Identified" neurons</span></h4>
<p>A neuron is called <i>identified</i> if it has properties that distinguish it from every other neuron in the same animal—properties such as location, neurotransmitter, gene expression pattern, and connectivity—and if every individual organism belonging to the same species has one and only one neuron with the same set of properties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hoyle_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hoyle-37">[37]</a></sup> In vertebrate nervous systems very few neurons are "identified" in this sense—in humans, there are believed to be none—but in simpler nervous systems, some or all neurons may be thus unique.  In the roundworm <i><a href="/wiki/Caenorhabditis_elegans" title="Caenorhabditis elegans">C. elegans</a></i>, whose nervous system is the most thoroughly described of any animal's, every neuron in the body is uniquely identifiable, with the same location and the same connections in every individual worm.  One notable consequence of this fact is that the form of the <i>C. elegans</i> nervous system is completely specified by the genome, with no experience-dependent plasticity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wormbook_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wormbook-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p>The brains of many molluscs and insects also contain substantial numbers of identified neurons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hoyle_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hoyle-37">[37]</a></sup> In vertebrates, the best known identified neurons are the gigantic <a href="/wiki/Mauthner_cell" title="Mauthner cell">Mauthner cells</a> of fish.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stein38_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stein38-38">[38]</a></sup> Every fish has two Mauthner cells, in the bottom part of the brainstem, one on the left side and one on the right.  Each Mauthner cell has an axon that crosses over, innervating neurons at the same brain level and then travelling down through the spinal cord, making numerous connections as it goes.  The synapses generated by a Mauthner cell are so powerful that a single action potential gives rise to a major behavioral response:  within milliseconds the fish curves its body into a <a href="/wiki/Mauthner_cell#The_C-start_behavior" title="Mauthner cell">C-shape</a>, then straightens, thereby propelling itself rapidly forward.  Functionally this is a fast escape response, triggered most easily by a strong sound wave or pressure wave impinging on the lateral line organ of the fish.  Mauthner cells are not the only identified neurons in fish—there are about 20 more types, including pairs of "Mauthner cell analogs" in each spinal segmental nucleus.  Although a Mauthner cell is capable of bringing about an escape response individually, in the context of ordinary behavior other types of cells usually contribute to shaping the amplitude and direction of the response.
</p><p>Mauthner cells have been described as <a href="/wiki/Command_neuron" title="Command neuron">command neurons</a>.  A command neuron is a special type of identified neuron, defined as a neuron that is capable of driving a specific behavior individually.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> Such neurons appear most commonly in the fast escape systems of various species—the <a href="/wiki/Squid_giant_axon" title="Squid giant axon">squid giant axon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Squid_giant_synapse" title="Squid giant synapse">squid giant synapse</a>, used for pioneering experiments in neurophysiology because of their enormous size, both participate in the fast escape circuit of the squid.  The concept of a command neuron has, however, become controversial, because of studies showing that some neurons that initially appeared to fit the description were really only capable of evoking a response in a limited set of circumstances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Simmons43_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Simmons43-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Function">Function</span></h2>
<p>At the most basic level, the function of the nervous system is to send signals from one cell to others, or from one part of the body to others. There are multiple ways that a cell can send signals to other cells. One is by releasing chemicals called <a href="/wiki/Hormone" title="Hormone">hormones</a> into the internal circulation, so that they can diffuse to distant sites. In contrast to this "broadcast" mode of signaling, the nervous system provides "point-to-point" signals—neurons project their axons to specific target areas and make synaptic connections with specific target cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gray170_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gray170-41">[41]</a></sup> Thus, neural signaling is capable of a much higher level of specificity than hormonal signaling. It is also much faster: the fastest nerve signals travel at speeds that exceed 100 meters per second.
</p><p>At a more integrative level, the primary function of the nervous system is to control the body.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KandelCh2_10-5"><a href="#cite_note-KandelCh2-10">[10]</a></sup> It does this by extracting information from the environment using sensory receptors, sending signals that encode this information into the central nervous system, processing the information to determine an appropriate response, and sending output signals to muscles or glands to activate the response. The evolution of a complex nervous system has made it possible for various animal species to have advanced perception abilities such as vision, complex social interactions, rapid coordination of organ systems, and integrated processing of concurrent signals. In humans, the sophistication of the nervous system makes it possible to have language, abstract representation of concepts, transmission of culture, and many other features of human society that would not exist without the human brain.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Neurons_and_synapses">Neurons and synapses</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chemical_synapse_schema_cropped.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="678" data-file-width="544" decoding="async" height="436" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Chemical_synapse_schema_cropped.jpg/350px-Chemical_synapse_schema_cropped.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Chemical_synapse_schema_cropped.jpg/525px-Chemical_synapse_schema_cropped.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/30/Chemical_synapse_schema_cropped.jpg 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chemical_synapse_schema_cropped.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Major elements in synaptic transmission.  An electrochemical wave called an <a href="/wiki/Action_potential" title="Action potential">action potential</a> travels along the <a href="/wiki/Axon" title="Axon">axon</a> of a <a href="/wiki/Neuron" title="Neuron">neuron</a>.  When the wave reaches a <a href="/wiki/Synapse" title="Synapse">synapse</a>, it provokes release of a small amount of <a href="/wiki/Neurotransmitter" title="Neurotransmitter">neurotransmitter</a> molecules, which bind to chemical receptor molecules in the membrane of the target cell.</div></div></div>
<p>Most neurons send signals via their <a href="/wiki/Axon" title="Axon">axons</a>, although some types are capable of dendrite-to-dendrite communication.  (In fact, the types of neurons called <a href="/wiki/Amacrine_cell" title="Amacrine cell">amacrine cells</a> have no axons, and communicate only via their dendrites.) Neural signals propagate along an axon in the form of electrochemical waves called <a href="/wiki/Action_potential" title="Action potential">action potentials</a>, which produce cell-to-cell signals at points where <a href="/wiki/Axon_terminal" title="Axon terminal">axon terminals</a> make <a href="/wiki/Synapse" title="Synapse">synaptic</a> contact with other cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KandelCh9_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-KandelCh9-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p><p>Synapses may be electrical or chemical.  <a href="/wiki/Electrical_synapse" title="Electrical synapse">Electrical synapses</a> make direct electrical connections between neurons,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hormuzdi_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hormuzdi-43">[43]</a></sup> but <a href="/wiki/Chemical_synapse" title="Chemical synapse">chemical synapses</a> are much more common, and much more diverse in function.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KandelCh10_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-KandelCh10-44">[44]</a></sup> At a chemical synapse, the cell that sends signals is called presynaptic, and the cell that receives signals is called postsynaptic.  Both the presynaptic and postsynaptic areas are full of molecular machinery that carries out the signalling process.  The presynaptic area contains large numbers of tiny spherical vessels called <a href="/wiki/Synaptic_vesicle" title="Synaptic vesicle">synaptic vesicles</a>, packed with <a href="/wiki/Neurotransmitter" title="Neurotransmitter">neurotransmitter</a> chemicals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KandelCh9_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-KandelCh9-42">[42]</a></sup> When the presynaptic terminal is electrically stimulated, an array of molecules embedded in the membrane are activated, and cause the contents of the vesicles to be released into the narrow space between the presynaptic and postsynaptic membranes, called the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Synaptic_cleft" title="Synaptic cleft">synaptic cleft</a>.  The neurotransmitter then binds to <a href="/wiki/Neurotransmitter_receptor" title="Neurotransmitter receptor">receptors</a> embedded in the postsynaptic membrane, causing them to enter an activated state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KandelCh10_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-KandelCh10-44">[44]</a></sup> Depending on the type of receptor, the resulting effect on the postsynaptic cell may be excitatory, inhibitory, or modulatory in more complex ways. For example, release of the neurotransmitter <a href="/wiki/Acetylcholine" title="Acetylcholine">acetylcholine</a> at a synaptic contact between a <a href="/wiki/Motor_neuron" title="Motor neuron">motor neuron</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Muscle_cell" title="Muscle cell">muscle cell</a> induces rapid contraction of the muscle cell.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KandelCh11_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-KandelCh11-45">[45]</a></sup> The entire synaptic transmission process takes only a fraction of a millisecond, although the effects on the postsynaptic cell may last much longer (even indefinitely, in cases where the synaptic signal leads to the formation of a <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Memory_trace&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Memory trace (page does not exist)">memory trace</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KandelCh4_18-2"><a href="#cite_note-KandelCh4-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p>
<table class="infobox">
<tbody><tr>
<th style="background-color:#d0d0d0;">Structure of a typical <a href="/wiki/Chemical_synapse" title="Chemical synapse">chemical synapse</a>
</th></tr>
<tr style="text-align: center">
<td><div style="position: relative;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SynapseSchematic_lines.svg"><img alt="An illustrated chemical synapse" data-file-height="1064" data-file-width="2000" decoding="async" height="213" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/SynapseSchematic_lines.svg/400px-SynapseSchematic_lines.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/SynapseSchematic_lines.svg/600px-SynapseSchematic_lines.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/SynapseSchematic_lines.svg/800px-SynapseSchematic_lines.svg.png 2x" width="400"/></a>
