</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>There are hundreds of different types of synapses.  In fact, there are over a hundred known neurotransmitters, and many of them have multiple types of receptors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KandelCh15_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-KandelCh15-46">[46]</a></sup> Many synapses use more than one neurotransmitter—a common arrangement is for a synapse to use one fast-acting small-molecule neurotransmitter such as <a href="/wiki/Glutamic_acid" title="Glutamic acid">glutamate</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gamma-Aminobutyric_acid" title="Gamma-Aminobutyric acid">GABA</a>, along with one or more <a href="/wiki/Peptide" title="Peptide">peptide</a> neurotransmitters that play slower-acting modulatory roles.  Molecular neuroscientists generally divide receptors into two broad groups:  <a href="/wiki/Ligand-gated_ion_channel" title="Ligand-gated ion channel">chemically gated ion channels</a> and <a href="/wiki/Second_messenger_system" title="Second messenger system">second messenger systems</a>.  When a chemically gated ion channel is activated, it forms a passage that allows specific types of ions to flow across the membrane.  Depending on the type of ion, the effect on the target cell may be excitatory or inhibitory.  When a second messenger system is activated, it starts a cascade of molecular interactions inside the target cell, which may ultimately produce a wide variety of complex effects, such as increasing or decreasing the sensitivity of the cell to stimuli, or even altering <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gene_transcription" title="Gene transcription">gene transcription</a>.
</p><p>According to a rule called <a href="/wiki/Dale%27s_principle" title="Dale's principle">Dale's principle</a>, which has only a few known exceptions, a neuron releases the same neurotransmitters at all of its synapses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Strata_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Strata-47">[47]</a></sup> This does not mean, though, that a neuron exerts the same effect on all of its targets, because the effect of a synapse depends not on the neurotransmitter, but on the receptors that it activates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KandelCh10_44-2"><a href="#cite_note-KandelCh10-44">[44]</a></sup> Because different targets can (and frequently do) use different types of receptors, it is possible for a neuron to have excitatory effects on one set of target cells, inhibitory effects on others, and complex modulatory effects on others still.  Nevertheless, it happens that the two most widely used neurotransmitters, <a href="/wiki/Glutamic_acid" title="Glutamic acid">glutamate</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gamma-Aminobutyric_acid" title="Gamma-Aminobutyric acid">GABA</a>, each have largely consistent effects.  Glutamate has several widely occurring types of receptors, but all of them are excitatory or modulatory.  Similarly, GABA has several widely occurring receptor types, but all of them are inhibitory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> Because of this consistency, glutamatergic cells are frequently referred to as "excitatory neurons", and GABAergic cells as "inhibitory neurons".  Strictly speaking, this is an abuse of terminology—it is the receptors that are excitatory and inhibitory, not the neurons—but it is commonly seen even in scholarly publications.
</p><p>One very important subset of synapses are capable of forming <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Memory_trace&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Memory trace (page does not exist)">memory traces</a> by means of long-lasting activity-dependent changes in synaptic strength.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Paradiso_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Paradiso-49">[49]</a></sup> The best-known form of neural memory is a process called <a href="/wiki/Long-term_potentiation" title="Long-term potentiation">long-term potentiation</a> (abbreviated LTP), which operates at synapses that use the neurotransmitter <a href="/wiki/Glutamic_acid" title="Glutamic acid">glutamate</a> acting on a special type of receptor known as the <a href="/wiki/NMDA_receptor" title="NMDA receptor">NMDA receptor</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cooke_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cooke-50">[50]</a></sup> The NMDA receptor has an "associative" property:  if the two cells involved in the synapse are both activated at approximately the same time, a channel opens that permits calcium to flow into the target cell.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bliss_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bliss-51">[51]</a></sup> The calcium entry initiates a second messenger cascade that ultimately leads to an increase in the number of glutamate receptors in the target cell, thereby increasing the effective strength of the synapse.  This change in strength can last for weeks or longer. Since the discovery of LTP in 1973, many other types of synaptic memory traces have been found, involving increases or decreases in synaptic strength that are induced by varying conditions, and last for variable periods of time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cooke_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cooke-50">[50]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Reward_system" title="Reward system">reward system</a>, that reinforces desired behaviour for example, depends on a variant form of LTP that is conditioned on an extra input coming from a reward-signalling pathway that uses <a href="/wiki/Dopamine" title="Dopamine">dopamine</a> as neurotransmitter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kauer_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kauer-52">[52]</a></sup> All these forms of synaptic modifiability, taken collectively, give rise to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neural_plasticity" title="Neural plasticity">neural plasticity</a>, that is, to a capability for the nervous system to adapt itself to variations in the environment.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Neural_circuits_and_systems">Neural circuits and systems</span></h3>
<p>The basic neuronal function of sending signals to other cells includes a capability for neurons to exchange signals with each other.  <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neural_network_(biological)" title="Neural network (biological)">Networks</a> formed by interconnected groups of neurons are capable of a wide variety of functions, including feature detection, pattern generation and timing,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dayan_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dayan-53">[53]</a></sup> and there are seen to be countless types of information processing possible. <a href="/wiki/Warren_Sturgis_McCulloch" title="Warren Sturgis McCulloch">Warren McCulloch</a> and <a href="/wiki/Walter_Pitts" title="Walter Pitts">Walter Pitts</a> showed in 1943 that even <a href="/wiki/Artificial_neural_network" title="Artificial neural network">artificial neural networks</a> formed from a greatly simplified mathematical abstraction of a neuron are capable of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Universal_computation" title="Universal computation">universal computation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McCullochPitts_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-McCullochPitts-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Descartes-reflex.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="442" data-file-width="418" decoding="async" height="233" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Descartes-reflex.JPG/220px-Descartes-reflex.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Descartes-reflex.JPG/330px-Descartes-reflex.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/8a/Descartes-reflex.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Descartes-reflex.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Illustration of pain pathway, from <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Descartes" title="René Descartes">René Descartes</a>'s <i>Treatise of Man</i></div></div></div>
<p>Historically, for many years the predominant view of the function of the nervous system was as a stimulus-response associator.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sherrington1906_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sherrington1906-55">[55]</a></sup> In this conception, neural processing begins with stimuli that activate sensory neurons, producing signals that propagate through chains of connections in the spinal cord and brain, giving rise eventually to activation of motor neurons and thereby to muscle contraction, i.e., to overt responses.  Descartes believed that all of the behaviors of animals, and most of the behaviors of humans, could be explained in terms of stimulus-response circuits, although he also believed that higher cognitive functions such as language were not capable of being explained mechanistically.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Descartes_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Descartes-56">[56]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Charles_Scott_Sherrington" title="Charles Scott Sherrington">Charles Sherrington</a>, in his influential 1906 book <i>The Integrative Action of the Nervous System</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sherrington1906_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sherrington1906-55">[55]</a></sup> developed the concept of stimulus-response mechanisms in much more detail, and <a href="/wiki/Behaviorism" title="Behaviorism">Behaviorism</a>, the school of thought that dominated <a href="/wiki/Psychology" title="Psychology">Psychology</a> through the middle of the 20th century, attempted to explain every aspect of human behavior in stimulus-response terms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baum_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Baum-57">[57]</a></sup>
</p><p>However, experimental studies of <a href="/wiki/Electrophysiology" title="Electrophysiology">electrophysiology</a>, beginning in the early 20th century and reaching high productivity by the 1940s, showed that the nervous system contains many mechanisms for maintaining <a href="/wiki/Membrane_potential#Cell_excitability" title="Membrane potential">cell excitability</a> and generating patterns of activity intrinsically, without requiring an external stimulus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Piccolino_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Piccolino-58">[58]</a></sup> Neurons were found to be capable of producing regular sequences of action potentials, or sequences of bursts, even in complete isolation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Johnston_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Johnston-59">[59]</a></sup> When intrinsically active neurons are connected to each other in complex circuits, the possibilities for generating intricate temporal patterns become far more extensive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dayan_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dayan-53">[53]</a></sup> A modern conception views the function of the nervous system partly in terms of stimulus-response chains, and partly in terms of intrinsically generated activity patterns—both types of activity interact with each other to generate the full repertoire of behavior.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Simmons_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Simmons-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Reflexes_and_other_stimulus-response_circuits">Reflexes and other stimulus-response circuits</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nervous_system_organization_en.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="482" data-file-width="601" decoding="async" height="321" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Nervous_system_organization_en.svg/400px-Nervous_system_organization_en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Nervous_system_organization_en.svg/600px-Nervous_system_organization_en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Nervous_system_organization_en.svg/800px-Nervous_system_organization_en.svg.png 2x" width="400"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nervous_system_organization_en.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Simplified schema of basic nervous system function: signals are picked up by sensory receptors and sent to the spinal cord and brain, where processing occurs that results in signals sent back to the spinal cord and then out to motor neurons</div></div></div>
<p>The simplest type of neural circuit is a <a href="/wiki/Reflex_arc" title="Reflex arc">reflex arc</a>, which begins with a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sensory_system" title="Sensory system">sensory</a> input and ends with a motor output, passing through a sequence of neurons connected in <a href="/wiki/Series_and_parallel_circuits" title="Series and parallel circuits">series</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KandelCh36_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-KandelCh36-61">[61]</a></sup> This can be shown in the "withdrawal reflex" causing a hand to jerk back after a hot stove is touched.  The circuit begins with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sensory_receptor" title="Sensory receptor">sensory receptors</a> in the skin that are activated by harmful levels of heat:  a special type of molecular structure embedded in the membrane causes heat to change the electrical field across the membrane.  If the change in electrical potential is large enough to pass the given threshold, it evokes an action potential, which is transmitted along the axon of the receptor cell, into the spinal cord.  There the axon makes excitatory synaptic contacts with other cells, some of which project (send axonal output) to the same region of the spinal cord, others projecting into the brain.  One target is a set of spinal <a href="/wiki/Interneuron" title="Interneuron">interneurons</a> that project to motor neurons controlling the arm muscles.  The interneurons excite the motor neurons, and if the excitation is strong enough, some of the motor neurons generate action potentials, which travel down their axons to the point where they make excitatory synaptic contacts with muscle cells.  The excitatory signals induce contraction of the muscle cells, which causes the joint angles in the arm to change, pulling the arm away.
</p><p>In reality, this straightforward schema is subject to numerous complications.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KandelCh36_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-KandelCh36-61">[61]</a></sup> Although for the simplest <a href="/wiki/Reflex" title="Reflex">reflexes</a> there are short neural paths from sensory neuron to motor neuron, there are also other nearby neurons that participate in the circuit and modulate the response.  Furthermore, there are projections from the brain to the spinal cord that are capable of enhancing or inhibiting the reflex.
</p><p>Although the simplest reflexes may be mediated by circuits lying entirely within the spinal cord, more complex responses rely on signal processing in the brain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KandelCh38_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-KandelCh38-62">[62]</a></sup> For example, when an object in the periphery of the visual field moves, and a person looks toward it many stages of signal processing are initiated.  The initial sensory response, in the retina of the eye, and the final motor response, in the oculomotor nuclei of the brain stem, are not all that different from those in a simple reflex, but the intermediate stages are completely different.  Instead of a one or two step chain of processing, the visual signals pass through perhaps a dozen stages of integration, involving the thalamus, cerebral cortex, basal ganglia, superior colliculus, cerebellum, and several brainstem nuclei.  These areas perform signal-processing functions that include <a href="/wiki/Feature_detection_(nervous_system)" title="Feature detection (nervous system)">feature detection</a>, <a href="/wiki/Perception" title="Perception">perceptual</a> analysis, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Memory_recall" title="Memory recall">memory recall</a>, <a href="/wiki/Decision-making" title="Decision-making">decision-making</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Motor_planning" title="Motor planning">motor planning</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KandelCh39_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-KandelCh39-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Feature_detection_(nervous_system)" title="Feature detection (nervous system)">Feature detection</a> is the ability to extract biologically relevant information from combinations of sensory signals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KandelCh21_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-KandelCh21-64">[64]</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Visual_system" title="Visual system">visual system</a>, for example, sensory receptors in the <a href="/wiki/Retina" title="Retina">retina</a> of the eye are only individually capable of detecting "points of light" in the outside world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KandelCh25_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-KandelCh25-65">[65]</a></sup> Second-level visual neurons receive input from groups of primary receptors, higher-level neurons receive input from groups of second-level neurons, and so on, forming a hierarchy of processing stages.  At each stage, important information is extracted from the signal ensemble and unimportant information is discarded.  By the end of the process, input signals representing "points of light" have been transformed into a neural representation of objects in the surrounding world and their properties.  The most sophisticated sensory processing occurs inside the brain, but complex feature extraction also takes place in the spinal cord and in peripheral sensory organs such as the retina.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Intrinsic_pattern_generation">Intrinsic pattern generation</span></h4>
<p>Although stimulus-response mechanisms are the easiest to understand, the nervous system is also capable of controlling the body in ways that do not require an external stimulus, by means of internally generated rhythms of activity. Because of the variety of voltage-sensitive ion channels that can be embedded in the membrane of a neuron, many types of neurons are capable, even in isolation, of generating rhythmic sequences of action potentials, or rhythmic alternations between high-rate bursting and quiescence. When neurons that are intrinsically rhythmic are connected to each other by excitatory or inhibitory synapses, the resulting networks are capable of a wide variety of dynamical behaviors, including <a href="/wiki/Attractor" title="Attractor">attractor</a> dynamics, periodicity, and even <a href="/wiki/Chaos_theory" title="Chaos theory">chaos</a>.  A network of neurons that uses its internal structure to generate temporally structured output, without requiring a corresponding temporally structured stimulus, is called a <a href="/wiki/Central_pattern_generator" title="Central pattern generator">central pattern generator</a>.
</p><p>Internal pattern generation operates on a wide range of time scales, from milliseconds to hours or longer.  One of the most important types of temporal pattern is <a href="/wiki/Circadian_rhythm" title="Circadian rhythm">circadian rhythmicity</a>—that is, rhythmicity with a period of approximately 24 hours.  All animals that have been studied show circadian fluctuations in neural activity, which control circadian alternations in behavior such as the sleep-wake cycle.  Experimental studies dating from the 1990s have shown that circadian rhythms are generated by a "genetic clock" consisting of a special set of genes whose expression level rises and falls over the course of the day.  Animals as diverse as insects and vertebrates share a similar genetic clock system.  The circadian clock is influenced by light but continues to operate even when light levels are held constant and no other external time-of-day cues are available.  The clock genes are expressed in many parts of the nervous system as well as many peripheral organs, but in mammals, all of these "tissue clocks" are kept in synchrony by signals that emanate from a master timekeeper in a tiny part of the brain called the <a href="/wiki/Suprachiasmatic_nucleus" title="Suprachiasmatic nucleus">suprachiasmatic nucleus</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mirror_neurons">Mirror neurons</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mirror_neuron" title="Mirror neuron">Mirror neuron</a></div>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Mirror_neuron" title="Mirror neuron">mirror neuron</a> is a neuron that <a href="/wiki/Action_potential" title="Action potential">fires</a> both when an animal acts and when the animal observes the same action performed by another.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RizzolattiCraighero2004_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-RizzolattiCraighero2004-66">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Keysers_2009_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Keysers_2009-67">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EmpathicBrain_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-EmpathicBrain-68">[68]</a></sup> Thus, the neuron "mirrors" the behavior of the other, as though the observer were itself acting. Such neurons have been directly observed in <a href="/wiki/Primate" title="Primate">primate</a> species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-architalbiol.org_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-architalbiol.org-69">[69]</a></sup> Birds have been shown to have imitative resonance behaviors and neurological evidence suggests the presence of some form of mirroring system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-architalbiol.org_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-architalbiol.org-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> In humans, brain activity consistent with that of mirror neurons has been found in the <a href="/wiki/Premotor_cortex" title="Premotor cortex">premotor cortex</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Supplementary_motor_area" title="Supplementary motor area">supplementary motor area</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Primary_somatosensory_cortex" title="Primary somatosensory cortex">primary somatosensory cortex</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Parietal_lobe" title="Parietal lobe">inferior parietal cortex</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Molenberghs_P,_Cunnington_R,_Mattingley_J_975–980_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-Molenberghs_P,_Cunnington_R,_Mattingley_J_975–980-71">[71]</a></sup> The function of the mirror system is a subject of much speculation. Many researchers in cognitive neuroscience and cognitive psychology consider that this system provides the physiological mechanism for the perception/action coupling (see the <a href="/wiki/Common_coding_theory" title="Common coding theory">common coding theory</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EmpathicBrain_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-EmpathicBrain-68">[68]</a></sup> They argue that mirror neurons may be important for understanding the actions of other people, and for learning new skills by imitation. Some researchers also speculate that mirror systems may simulate observed actions, and thus contribute to <a href="/wiki/Theory_of_mind" title="Theory of mind">theory of mind</a> skills,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> while others relate mirror neurons to <a href="/wiki/Language" title="Language">language</a> abilities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> However, to date, no widely accepted neural or computational models have been put forward to describe how mirror neuron activity supports cognitive functions such as imitation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dinstein_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dinstein-75">[75]</a></sup> There are neuroscientists who caution that the claims being made for the role of mirror neurons are not supported by adequate research.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ncbi.nlm.nih.gov_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-ncbi.nlm.nih.gov-76">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-else.econ.ucl.ac.uk_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-else.econ.ucl.ac.uk-77">[77]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Development">Development</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Development_of_the_nervous_system" title="Development of the nervous system">Development of the nervous system</a> and <a href="/wiki/Development_of_the_nervous_system_in_humans" title="Development of the nervous system in humans">Development of the nervous system in humans</a></div>
<p>In vertebrates, landmarks of embryonic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neural_development" title="Neural development">neural development</a> include the <a href="/wiki/Neurogenesis" title="Neurogenesis">birth</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cellular_differentiation" title="Cellular differentiation">differentiation</a> of <a href="/wiki/Neuron" title="Neuron">neurons</a> from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stem_cells" title="Stem cells">stem cell</a> precursors, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cellular_migration" title="Cellular migration">migration</a> of immature neurons from their birthplaces in the embryo to their final positions, outgrowth of <a href="/wiki/Axon" title="Axon">axons</a> from neurons and <a href="/wiki/Axon_guidance" title="Axon guidance">guidance</a> of the motile <a href="/wiki/Growth_cone" title="Growth cone">growth cone</a> through the embryo towards postsynaptic partners, the generation of <a href="/wiki/Synapse" title="Synapse">synapses</a> between these axons and their postsynaptic partners, and finally the lifelong <a href="/wiki/Synaptic_plasticity" title="Synaptic plasticity">changes</a> in synapses which are thought to underlie learning and memory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KandelCh52_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-KandelCh52-78">[78]</a></sup>
</p><p>All bilaterian animals at an early stage of development form a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gastrula" title="Gastrula">gastrula</a>, which is polarized, with one end called the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Animal_pole" title="Animal pole">animal pole</a> and the other the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vegetal_pole" title="Vegetal pole">vegetal pole</a>.  The gastrula has the shape of a disk with three layers of cells, an inner layer called the <a href="/wiki/Endoderm" title="Endoderm">endoderm</a>, which gives rise to the lining of most internal organs, a middle layer called the <a href="/wiki/Mesoderm" title="Mesoderm">mesoderm</a>, which gives rise to the bones and muscles, and an outer layer called the <a href="/wiki/Ectoderm" title="Ectoderm">ectoderm</a>, which gives rise to the skin and nervous system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SanesCh1_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-SanesCh1-79">[79]</a></sup>
</p>
<table align="center">
<tbody><tr>
<td><div class="center"><div class="thumb tnone"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gray17.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="426" data-file-width="400" decoding="async" height="266" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Gray17.png/250px-Gray17.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Gray17.png/375px-Gray17.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/95/Gray17.png 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gray17.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Human embryo, showing neural groove</div></div></div></div>
</td>
<td><div class="center"><div class="thumb tnone"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:427px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Development_of_the_neural_tube.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="368" data-file-width="598" decoding="async" height="262" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Development_of_the_neural_tube.png/425px-Development_of_the_neural_tube.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/4c/Development_of_the_neural_tube.png 1.5x" width="425"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Development_of_the_neural_tube.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Four stages in the development of the neural tube in the human embryo</div></div></div></div>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>In vertebrates, the first sign of the nervous system is the appearance of a thin strip of cells along the center of the back, called the <a href="/wiki/Neural_plate" title="Neural plate">neural plate</a>.  The inner portion of the neural plate (along the midline) is destined to become the <a href="/wiki/Central_nervous_system" title="Central nervous system">central nervous system</a> (CNS), the outer portion the <a href="/wiki/Peripheral_nervous_system" title="Peripheral nervous system">peripheral nervous system</a> (PNS).  As development proceeds, a fold called the <a href="/wiki/Neural_groove" title="Neural groove">neural groove</a> appears along the midline.  This fold deepens, and then closes up at the top.  At this point the future CNS appears as a cylindrical structure called the <a href="/wiki/Neural_tube" title="Neural tube">neural tube</a>, whereas the future PNS appears as two strips of tissue called the <a href="/wiki/Neural_crest" title="Neural crest">neural crest</a>, running lengthwise above the neural tube.  The sequence of stages from neural plate to neural tube and neural crest is known as <a href="/wiki/Neurulation" title="Neurulation">neurulation</a>.
</p><p>In the early 20th century, a set of famous experiments by Hans Spemann and Hilde Mangold showed that the formation of nervous tissue is "induced" by signals from a group of mesodermal cells called the <i>organizer region</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KandelCh52_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-KandelCh52-78">[78]</a></sup> For decades, though, the nature of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neural_induction" title="Neural induction">neural induction</a> defeated every attempt to figure it out, until finally it was resolved by genetic approaches in the 1990s.  Induction of neural tissue requires inhibition of the gene for a so-called <a href="/wiki/Bone_morphogenetic_protein" title="Bone morphogenetic protein">bone morphogenetic protein</a>, or BMP.  Specifically the protein <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/BMP4" title="BMP4">BMP4</a> appears to be involved.  Two proteins called <a href="/wiki/Noggin_(protein)" title="Noggin (protein)">Noggin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chordin" title="Chordin">Chordin</a>, both secreted by the mesoderm, are capable of inhibiting BMP4 and thereby inducing ectoderm to turn into neural tissue.  It appears that a similar molecular mechanism is involved for widely disparate types of animals, including arthropods as well as vertebrates.  In some animals, however, another type of molecule called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fibroblast_Growth_Factor" title="Fibroblast Growth Factor">Fibroblast Growth Factor</a> or FGF may also play an important role in induction.
</p><p>Induction of neural tissues causes formation of neural precursor cells, called <a href="/wiki/Neuroblast" title="Neuroblast">neuroblasts</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KandelCh53_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-KandelCh53-80">[80]</a></sup> In drosophila, neuroblasts divide asymmetrically, so that one product is a "ganglion mother cell" (GMC), and the other is a neuroblast.  A GMC divides once, to give rise to either a pair of neurons or a pair of glial cells.  In all, a neuroblast is capable of generating an indefinite number of neurons or glia.
</p><p>As shown in a 2008 study, one factor common to all <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Symmetry_(biology)" title="Symmetry (biology)">bilateral</a> organisms (including humans) is a family of secreted <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Signalling_molecule" title="Signalling molecule">signaling molecules</a> called <a href="/wiki/Neurotrophin" title="Neurotrophin">neurotrophins</a> which regulate the growth and survival of <a href="/wiki/Neuron" title="Neuron">neurons</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zhu_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zhu-81">[81]</a></sup> Zhu et al. identified DNT1, the first neurotrophin found in <a href="/wiki/Fly" title="Fly">flies</a>. DNT1 shares structural similarity with all known neurotrophins and is a key factor in the fate of neurons in <a href="/wiki/Drosophila" title="Drosophila">Drosophila</a>. Because neurotrophins have now been identified in both vertebrate and invertebrates, this evidence suggests that neurotrophins were present in an ancestor common to bilateral organisms and may represent a common mechanism for nervous system formation.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Pathology">Pathology</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Neurology" title="Neurology">Neurology</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Psychiatry" title="Psychiatry">Psychiatry</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:269px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CNS_protection.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1080" data-file-width="1920" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/CNS_protection.jpg/267px-CNS_protection.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/CNS_protection.jpg/401px-CNS_protection.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/CNS_protection.jpg/534px-CNS_protection.jpg 2x" width="267"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CNS_protection.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Layers protecting the brain and spinal cord.</div></div></div>
<p>The central nervous system is protected by major physical and chemical barriers.  Physically, the brain and spinal cord are surrounded by tough <a href="/wiki/Meninges" title="Meninges">meningeal</a> membranes, and enclosed in the bones of the <a href="/wiki/Skull" title="Skull">skull</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vertebral_column" title="Vertebral column">vertebral column</a>, which combine to form a strong physical shield.  Chemically, the brain and spinal cord are isolated by the <a href="/wiki/Blood%E2%80%93brain_barrier" title="Blood–brain barrier">blood–brain barrier</a>, which prevents most types of chemicals from moving from the bloodstream into the interior of the CNS.  These protections make the CNS less susceptible in many ways than the PNS; the flip side, however, is that damage to the CNS tends to have more serious consequences.
</p><p>Although nerves tend to lie deep under the skin except in a few places such as the <a href="/wiki/Ulnar_nerve" title="Ulnar nerve">ulnar nerve</a> near the elbow joint, they are still relatively exposed to physical damage, which can cause pain, loss of sensation, or loss of muscle control.  Damage to nerves can also be caused by swelling or bruises at places where a nerve passes through a tight bony channel, as happens in <a href="/wiki/Carpal_tunnel_syndrome" title="Carpal tunnel syndrome">carpal tunnel syndrome</a>.  If a nerve is completely transected, it will often <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nerve_regeneration" title="Nerve regeneration">regenerate</a>, but for long nerves this process may take months to complete.  In addition to physical damage, <a href="/wiki/Peripheral_neuropathy" title="Peripheral neuropathy">peripheral neuropathy</a> may be caused by many other medical problems, including genetic conditions, metabolic conditions such as <a href="/wiki/Diabetes" title="Diabetes">diabetes</a>, inflammatory conditions such as <a href="/wiki/Guillain%E2%80%93Barr%C3%A9_syndrome" title="Guillain–Barré syndrome">Guillain–Barré syndrome</a>, vitamin deficiency, infectious diseases such as <a href="/wiki/Leprosy" title="Leprosy">leprosy</a> or <a href="/wiki/Shingles" title="Shingles">shingles</a>, or poisoning by toxins such as heavy metals.  Many cases have no cause that can be identified, and are referred to as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Idiopathic" title="Idiopathic">idiopathic</a>.  It is also possible for nerves to lose function temporarily, resulting in numbness as stiffness—common causes include mechanical pressure, a drop in temperature, or chemical interactions with <a href="/wiki/Local_anesthetic" title="Local anesthetic">local anesthetic</a> drugs such as <a href="/wiki/Lidocaine" title="Lidocaine">lidocaine</a>.
</p><p>Physical damage to the spinal cord may result in <a href="/wiki/Paralysis" title="Paralysis">loss of sensation or movement</a>.  If an injury to the spine produces nothing worse than swelling, the symptoms may be transient, but if nerve fibers in the spine are actually destroyed, the loss of function is usually permanent.  Experimental studies have shown that spinal nerve fibers attempt to regrow in the same way as nerve fibers, but in the spinal cord, tissue destruction usually produces scar tissue that cannot be penetrated by the regrowing nerves.
</p>

<li id="cite_note-48"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-48">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">There are a number of exceptional situations in which GABA has been found to have excitatory effects, mainly during early development.  For a review see <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFMartyLlano2005">Marty A, Llano I (June 2005).
